<plain_text><page sequence="1">Eightieth Critical Bibliography OF THE HISTORY OF SCIENCE AND ITS CULTURAL INFLUENCES (TO 1 JANUARY 1954) The last Critical Bibliography to appear was C. B. 79, published in ISIS, 1953, 44: 102-204. In the foreword to it, Dr. George Sarton ex- plained that it was the last one to be compiled by him. The present Critical Bibliography was assembled by I. Bernard Cohen and Katharine Strelsky, with the cooperation of: ALLEN CABANISS, University of Missouri CAROLYN EIsEuE, New York DONALD FLEM1NG, Providence, R. I. SoLoMoN GANDZ (t) C. DoRs HELLMAN, New York LUCILE E. HOYME, Washington, D. C. DAvm JoNAH, Providence, R. I. THOMAS S. KuHN, Cambridge, Mass. CHAUNCEY D. LEAKE, University of Texas WYNDHAM D. MILES, Edgewood, Maryland WALTER PAGEL, London, England P. VAN DER PAS, South Pasadena, California JEAN PELSENEER, Brussels, Belgium LADIsLAO RETI, Buenos Aires, Argentina GEORGE SARTON, Cambridge, Mass. W. STEPHEN THOMAS, Rochester, New York SONIA S. WOHL, New York CONWAY ZmKrS, Philadelphia, Pa. and with the assistance of the Editorial Commit- tee for the publication of ISIS (MARSHALL CLA- GETT, I. E. DRABKIN, JOHaN F. FULTON, HENRY GUERLAC, and CONWAY ZIRKLE). The method of classification differs from that used in former Critical Bibliographies, and was proposed by a committee appointed by the Coun- cil of the History of Science Society (Henry Guerlac, chairman, and I. B. Cohen, Morris Lei- kind, Genevieve Miller, Dorothy Schullian, Con- way Zirkle). This classification is explained in H. Guerlac: "A proposed revision of the ISIS Critical Bibliography" (Isis, 1953, 44: 226-231). For the benefit of the users of the Critical Bibli- ography, the main features may be summarized briefly as follows. There are four main sections: A - History of Science: General References and Tools B - Science and Its History from Special Points of View C-Histories of the Special Sciences D - Chronological Classification. The divisions and subdivisions of each of these main sections are listed below in the Table of Contents. A history of technology, or of medi- cine, etc., in any one of the chronological periods (e.g., classical antiquity, medieval Islam, or the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries) would be listed in Section D, under the subdivision Tech- nology or Medicine, etc., in the appropriate chronological section; it would not, therefore, be listed a second time under Section C in the divi- sion of Technology or Medicine and the Medical Sciences, etc. But a work dealing with the his- tory of technology or of medicine, etc., in gen- eral, or a work on such a topic that covered two or more chronological periods of Section D, would be entered in Section C under the ap- propriate division, Technology, or Medicine, etc. A general history of the Royal Society of Lon- don would be found under HS II, Scientific In- stitutions, in Section B, but an article dealing with the founding of the Royal Society would be found under HS 37 Seventeenth and Eight- eenth Centuries in Section D. Some topics necessarily overlap, so that users of the Critical Bibliography must examine all categories of possible relation to their work. For example, articles on microscopes might be listed under HS 24 Biology, HS 26 Medicine and the Medical Sciences, or HS 22 Physical Sciences, in Section C, or under one of these subdivisions in Section D, depending on the nature of the par- ticular work. Similarly, articles or books dealing with anatomy and physiology might be classified under either HS 25 Biological Sciences, HS 26 Medicine and the Medical Sciences in Section C, or under one of these subdivisions of Section D, depending on the nature of the subject. All bibliographical citations have been made according to the style of the World List of Sci- entific Periodicals published in the years Igoo- z950, third edition, edited by William Allan Smith (British Museum), and Francis Lawrence Kent (Unesco), assisted by George Burder Strat- ton (Zoological Society of London), New York: Academic Press; London: Butterworth Scientific Publications, 1952. A partial list compiled along similar lines was published by the World Health Organization and Unesco in 1953 under the title World Medical Periodicals. Among the features of this system are: "Nouns have capital, and adjectives small initial letters, except in English, when the adjectival forms from proper names retain the capital. In English, adjectives used as nouns and nouns used as adjectives also have capital initial letters. Prepositions and articles are normally omitted. The conjunction 'and' (with the corresponding words in other lan- guages) is omitted, except in titles consisting of only two names connected by 'and' and where it connects broken compounds." Where possible, cognate words in all languages are reduced to the same form. Thus, Academy, Acadimie, Academia are all rendered Acad.; Society, Soci&amp;t6, Societh, Sociedad, Sociedade, Societas, Societate, Societiat, as Soc. All titles with single words are not ab- breviated (contrary to the Index Gesch. Med. III</page><page sequence="2">I I2 HISTORY OF SCIENCE, GENERAL WORKS Naturwiss. Tech., in which ISIS becomes Is.). This system will be found easy to use; in cases of doubt, if any, the World List presents almost all journals devoted to science and the history of science in alphabetical order. Authors of articles and books related to the history of science and its cultural influences are requested to send to the editor of ISIS a copy of all books, pamphlets, and papers so that they may be included in these Crtical Bibliographies, and eventually reviewed and discussed. In this way they will help all others interested in the history of science and make sure that the fruits of their research will become part of the litera- ture used by others. Owing to the termination of Dr. Sarton's work on these Critical Bibliogra- phies, certain important works may have escaped our attention; we would be grateful to receive copies of them for inclusion in the Eighty-first Critical Bibliography now under preparation. The Eightieth Critical Bibliography contains ap- proximately 2ioo entries and includes articles in journals printed before i January 1954, as well as some books published in I954. I. The need for a new historical atlas has long been felt, and the present first instalments of a new three- volume work suggests that this may now be ful- filled. In addition to cultural and political maps of antiquity, there are many plans of the major ancient cities showing the location of important structures. The plan of Rome at the time of the Caesars is printed on transparent paper so that comparison may be made with Rome of 1950, printed on the next page. There is an excellent geographisches Suchregister by Hermann Bengtson. I. B. C. HAYEK, F. A. (editor). Capitalism and the Historians. vii + I88 pp. Chicago: Univer- sity of Chicago Press, I954. $3.00. The present book consists of an introductory essay by Professor Hayek and four principal essays by T. S. Ashton, L. M. Hacker, W. H. Hutt, and Bertrand de Jouvenel. Their common thesis is that historians and social scientists have conspired to malign capitalism, particularly as it manifested it- self in early 1gth-century England. At least one of the essays, that by Professor Hutt, originally pub- lished in 1926, ought never to have been exhumed. It turns in considerable part on the thesis that there is nothing disingenuous in a physician's refusing to say in advance that a 23-hour day would be injuri- ous to the health of children. The essay by Pro- fessor Ashton, the well-known historian of the In- dustrial Revolution, is a temperate defence of the view that many, though not all, members of the working class in England were benefited by the coming of the factory system. But the most stimu- lating contribution is that of Bertrand de Jouvenel, in which he analyzes with great subtlety why in- tellectuals are normally hostile to men of business, and comes close to vindicating the intellectuals. The net result therefore of the only essays of any great value is to dilute the central thesis of the book and dissipate the polemical tone assumed by the other authors. D. F. An illustrated review of the history of printed pictures considered in part as "communications of fact" rather than as "works of art." JASPERS, KARL. The origin and goal of his- tory. Xvi + 294 pp. New Haven: Yale Uni- versity Press, 1953. $4.00. The author holds that "science is indeed the hall- mark of our era, but in a shape in which it ceases to be science." Science is said to have "three indis- pensable traits: it is methodological cognition, co- gently certain and universally valid." The challeng- ing statements of Jaspers on science and technology as the new elements in history that must be encom- passed in an over-all philosophy of history are some- what hard to interpret, because of his theologico- philosophical approach to science in general terms, unilluminated by meaningful examples. PHILOSOPHY OF SCIENCE AND METHODS OF SCIENCE (General works only; those devoted to a special period or person will be found below under D.) BERTALOFFY, LUDWIG voN. Philosophy of science in scientific education. Sci. Mon., I953, 77: 233-239- BLACK, MAX. Problems of analysis. Philo- sophical essays. xi + 304 pp. Ithaca; Cornell University Press, I954. $5.oo. In this volume Professor Black of Cornell has reprinted a number of his essays dealing with logic and the philosophy of science, and has added some new oncs. BRAITHWAITE, RICHARD BEVAN. Scien- tific explanation. A study of the function of theory, probability and law in science. Based upon the Tarner Lectures, I946. x + 376 pp. Cambridge: at the University Press, 1953. $8.00. Scientific Explanation is an elegant, stimulating, and readable study of the logical structure and verification of scientific theories written by the new Professor of Moral Philosophy at Cambridge. In- debted but not addicted to the more fragmentary ground-breaking discussions of Ramsey, Wald, and Neyman, this book is the most complete and original treatment of its field in recent years. By insisting that in scientific practice the "observed fact" is irreducible, Braithwaite restricts his atten- tion to problems which philosophically alert scien- tists can recognize in their own work and rejects the vexing problems of the philosophy of percep- tion which have dissipated so many studies in this field. Some philosophers will find this premise ingenuous, but there is nothing naive about the way it is exploited. Throughout the discussion the author combines rigor and authority with clarity by the application of symbolic methods to well- selected elementary paradigms. And his results are frequently striking. Particularly important is the proof, in chapter 3, that the concepts employed in a scientific theory cannot be operationally defined without robbing them of their applicability to new situations. The point may be trivial to the historian of science, but it has not been so to the philosopher, and Braithwaite's symbolic formulation and dis- cussion give his point new depth and consequence. There are other important insights to be gained from this rewarding book, whose wealth defies paraphrase beyond that provided by a careful read- ing of the title and sub-title. T. S. K. BRONOWSKI, J. In an introductory chapter (2: "The august author: his interest in science") it is stated that "the Holy Father . . . was the first to warn the world of the dangers of atomic energy," that in I943 "he foreshadowed the com- ing of the atom bomb." All will be grateful for an English version of this important document, setting forth a harmony between the scientist's quest and the doctrines of the Roman Church in a major document for students of the relations between science and religion. It is interesting to observe that the pope's statements are apt to be more ac- curate than those of the commentator, e.g., the pope refers to the Bohr theory but the commentator con- siders the atom as a "solar system in miniature"; the pope refers to the "problems" of cosmic rays, but the commentator calls cosmic rays "rays of energy," and suggests that they are "the cause of cancer"; nor does the pope ever imply that "a leveling tendency" in "all physical systems" is "as if Nature had democratic leanings." HUGHES, ARTHUR. Science in English en- cyclopaedias, I704-I875. III: Meteorology. Ann. Sci., I953, 9: 233-264.</page><page sequence="20">130 BIOLOGY HS 2&amp; BIOLOGICAL SCIENCES. (Zoology, botany, anatomy, physiology, physical anthropology; also applied biology, including certain aspects of agriculture, such as plant and animal breeding, economic entomology, etc.) a. Biology in general. ARBER, AGNES. The mind and the eye: a study of the biologist's standpoint. 126 pp. New York: Cambridge University Press, I953. Reviewed by Conway Zirkle in Isis, 1953, 44: 300-301. BAKER, JOHN R. The cell-theory: a restate- ment, history, and critique. Pt. I, Quart. J. microscop. Sci., 1948, 89: 103-125. Pt. II, ibid., 1949, go: 87-I08, 331. Pt. III, ibid., 1952, 93: I67-190. Pt. IV, ibid., 1953, 94: 407-440. These incisive, well written, fully documented, illustrated articles are based on an extensive exami- nation of original sources without neglecting major secondary works. The author has stated the cell- theory in a series of seven propositions, of which the articles listed above illustrate the first three: (i) Most organisms contain or consist of a large number of microscopical bodies called 'cells,' which, in the less differentiated tissues, tend to be polyhedral or nearly spherical. (2) Cells have certain definable characters; these characters show that cells (a) are all of essentially the same nature and (b) are units of structure. (3) Cells always arise, directly or in- directly, from pre-existing cells, usually by binary fission. "The diversity of views about the meaning of the expression 'cell-theory' makes it evident that the truth or untruth of the theory can only be estab- lished if it can be formulated in clear terms. The different parts of the cell-theory do not necessarily stand or fall together, and the formulation should therefore be in a series of separate propositions, each of which can be examined independently." In "con- sidering the evidence for and against each proposi- tion" the author found "it necessary to discover how the various bodies of opinion originated and devel- oped." These articles contain much new information on the history of the cell-theory and useful critical cor- rectives respecting historical material already in print in standard works; all of which whets the historian's appetite for the completion of the series and its eventual rcpublication (perhaps in expanded form) as a book. "The history of microscopy is inseparably bound up with the optical possibilities of the successive types of microscopes, rather than with their me- chanical construction. For this reason, the present catalogue contains, in addition to the description of simple microscopes, also their optical properties." The author's collection of data covering the focal length, magnifying power, aperture, resolving power, and field view of these microscopes, or "loupes," provides a basis for understanding the kind of research that could be done with them. After describing goniometers and other forms of craniometers used in human measurements in the seventeenth, eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, the author concludes: "Although there were earlier examples of the application of anthropometry to practical problems, the most notable being Bertillon's (I893) system of identification, it was not until after I910 that anthropometry was used as a technique in a wide range of fields. Emmons' (1913) study on the pelvimetry of Indian women, the studies on child growth carried out by such organizations as the Iowa State Child Welfare Research Station, Matieg- ka's (1I921) studies on physical efficiency, Franzen's (1929) studies on obesity are only a few examples of the increasing emphasis on practical applications, which reached their culmination in the Army an- thropometry of World War II and succeeding years." The Black Madonna: An example of cultural borrowing. Sci. Mon., I953, 76: 3I9-324. Many of the statues of the Virgin Mary depict her as black. Some of these can be explained readily as due to unpremeditated physical causes such as the paint pigment deteriorating, etc. In certain re- gions such statues are merely the color of the in- digenous population. There remain some black Madonnas, however, whose color can not be ex- plained so easily. These are found in many Euro- pean countries and their black color is not acci- dental. The authors find that these Madonnas are black because of ancient cultural borrowings, inas- much as they have taken over in part the local functionings of the Egyptian Isis and the Greek Demeter. Black was the ancient color of the god- dess of the underworld. C. Z. OAKLEY, KENNETH P. Dating the Libyan desert silica glass. Nature, I952, I70: 447-449. I fig. POLSON, C. J.; BRITTAIN, R. Hispanic-American contribution to the history of scurvy. 8 fac- similes. Centaurus, ig5o, I: I2-23. "The Hispanic-American contribution to the his- tory of scurvy is of the utmost importance. Since 1579 Farfan recommended the juice of citrus fruits for the treatment of the ulcers of the gums undoubt- edly of scorbutic origin; recommendations which he again proposed in his Mexican books in 1592 and 161o. In I623 in Lisbon, Abreu talks about scurvy and its treatment in Mexico in 1712, half a century before Lind. On the other hand, the treatises of Lardizabal, which were printed in Spain in 1764 for the Society of Caracas in Venezuela, and that of Corbella, ancient Lieutenant physician of Tucuman, La Plata River, and also of Paraguay, printed in Madrid 1794, give important information. The latter one points out that scurvy is a disease due to the use of an improper diet in which some essential factors are lacking." GUITARD, E.-H. Le prestigieux passe des eaux minerales. Sci., Japan, I95I, no. 20, Oct. HEYMAN, R. The development of the ar- moured battleship. Act. Congr. int. Hist. Sci., I953, 7 372-376. HODGEN, MARGARET T. Change and his- tory. A study of the dated distributions of technological innovations in England. (Viking Fund Publications in Anthropology, No. M8.) 324 pp., 5 tables, map series i-io. New York: Wenner-Gren Foundation for Anthropological Research, Inc., 1952. $4.50. A contribution to the history of technological change, dealing with innovations and the dated dis- tribution of technological innovations, primarily in Britain. "This inquiry has been conducted on the premise that it is useless to argue that the method of science is inapplicable to the dated records of man's life without first making an attempt and then judging the results. It has a dual purpose. It is in- tended to shed light on the temporal and geographi- cal incidence of technological changes, but much more important, to offer geographical evidence of the recurrence and classifiability of dated events and their consequent responsiveness to scientific method." A third of the book is given to charts of technological innovations and to maps showing their distribution. "The temporal classification and mapping of the re- current events of innovation clearly show that while they took place in a surprisingly small fraction of the total number of English parishes, they made their appearance, from the Norman Conquest on- ward, in certain definite regions and in the course of three saltations, surges, or accelerations. The first of these began in the twelfth century with 89 parish sites of innovation, and it continued at a steep gradient into the fourteenth with 375 parish sites of innovation. The second, beginning in the sixteenth century with 505 parishes, continued at a reduced rate into the seventeenth, with 57I par- ishes. While the third, usually called the Industrial Revolution, began in the eighteenth century (or possibly in the seventeenth century) with 833 parishes, and may not yet have run its course (Appendix, Table 2)." A history of the problems of technology presented in selections from primary sources from antiquity to the present. Where literary documents are not available, as in the history of the harness and horse collar, Klemm uses graphic illustrations and writes a brief account of the subject. Each selection (in German translation) is introduced by an explanatory commentary. There are many beautiful illustra- tions, an exemplary bibliography, a chronological table and a complete index. The early part of the book, as a result of the method of composition, cannot treat of primitive, ancient and preclassical technology. A practical manual and general introduction to the subject, explaining what the archeologist does, how he does it, and why. There is a splendid his- torical introduction, and there are historical refer- ences in the other sections. Says Sir Mortimer (p. 4I): "The first recorded instance, so far as I am aware, of the observation of archaeological stratifica- tion related not to an occupation-site but to a burial mound. At the time when the episode oc- curred, the word stratification, in the sense in which I have just used it, was not yet in circulation, but the elements of the matter were precociously appre- ciated by that remarkable man, Thomas Jefferson, whose major title to fame is, I suppose, that he was third President of the United States and an author of the Declaration of American Independ- ence. For us he may rank in the lesser but not negligible role of the first scientific digger." WOOLLEY, SIR LEONARD. Spadework in archaeology. 124 pp., frontispiece, 14 ill. Queen Ichetis' wheat; a contribution to the study of early dynastic Emmer of Egypt. Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab, Biologiske meddelel- ser, I953, 21: no. 8, I7 pp., 4 pl. During the excavation of Queen Ichetis' (Sixth Dynasty) tomb in the Saqqarah Pyramid, Jean- Philippe Lauer discovered a grain deposit. Apply- ing the methods of plant husbandry archeology, the present writer examined and described a sample of 2 wholes and 27 major portions of mummified wheat, approximately 4500 years old. He identifies the Ichetis wheat as Emmer, T. dicoccum, feeling that while certain dimensional conformities with recent Eicorn exist, morphological discrepancies are too great for referring any part of the find to this species. The horoscope of Ceionius Rufius Albinus. Amer. J. Philology, I953, 74: 4i8-420. NEUGEBAUER, 0. The chronology of Vettius Valens' Anthologiae. Harvard Theological Rev., I954, 47: 65-67. "The mutual independence of the Anthology and the Tetrabiblos appears now in a new light. Ptole- my's own observations, quoted in the Almagest, range between A.D. 127 and I4I. Since the Tetra- biblos, by its own statement, was written after the Almagest, one may well imagine that the two most influential astrological treatises were written practically simultaneously: Vettius Valens eagerly collecting material from his files of 'case histories' to confirm and refine the ever growing doctrine, Ptolemy shaping a consistent theory of what ap- peared to him to be a universal science of life. "But more important than the question of priority is the problem of the early chronology of Hellenistic and Roman astrology. It is now clear that the direct sources of Vettius Valens do not lead back beyond the middle of the first century A.D. This, by and large, is the same limit which still exists for us today for actually preserved horoscopes on papyrus." PANNEKOEK, A. The astronomical system of Herakleides. Koninklijke ned. Akad. Wet., Proc., series B, I952, 55: 373-38i. (Circular no. 4 of the Astronomical Institute of the Uni- versity of Amsterdam.) "Thus it appears that in all the texts preserved from antiquity there is no proof nor even an indica- tion that Herakleides should have put forward an orbital motion of the earth. What is presented as a world system of Herakleides by Schiaparelli or by Van der Waerden is a theoretical construction, presumed to be the only logical or possible conse- quence of former knowledge, but not sustained by fact. To us who look from later times and know all further developments as simple truths, their dis- covery may seem to have been easy; so we expect from a thinker, who by two discoveries has shown his ingenuity, that he should have made the next step also. We do not realize, then, what a large mental effort of the best brains has been necessary to perform in the progress of science each single step. The two discoveries righdy ascribed to Herakleides, the rotation of the earth and the orbit of Venus about the sun, constitute such an impor- tant progress compared with the former and preva- lent views, that they suffice to give him a lasting place in the history of science." A middle English translation of Macer floridus de viribus herbarum. 338 pp. Inaugural Dissertation. Uppsala, 1949. Eight MSS of an Eiglish translation of Dc viribus herbarum are known to exist, seven in England and one in Sweden. Six of the MSS are probably I5th-century or later, but two (one in the British Museum and the one in Stockholm) are earlier. The Swedish MS is the more complete of the two. Dc viribus herbarum, a Latin poem of 2269 lines, was composed between 849 and III2. If the author was Odo de Meung, as is generally believed, it dates from the iith century. The interests of Dr. Frisk are primarily linguistic. The English trans- lation he prints was made into the dialect of four- teenth-century Oxford. Alternate readings from the other MSS are included and also a very com- plete vocabulary. Incidentally the work should be of great interest to historians of botany and pharma- cology. C. Z. History of the great Islamic universi- ties. 338 pp. in Arabic. Tetuin: D&amp;r al-tab&amp;'a al-maghriblya, I953. This book, which received the Miilay al-1:asan Prize in 1952, is an elaborate history of Islamic in- stitutes of higher learning. The word "universities" used for convenience with the title is misleading, for those Islamic universities were very different from our Western ones. The author does not deal separately with the main schools of the Islamic world, but discusses their purposes, organization, methods, and traditions in a general way. The main schools are enumerated in the first chapter, how- ever: first, those of Medina and Mecca, then those of Basra, Kiifa, Fustia (old Cairo), Jerusalem, Damas- cus, Tunis, Qairwan, Baghdad, C6rdoba, Fas, Cairo (al-Azhar), Najaf. A final chapter deals with the education of women from the time of the Prophet until our own day. In his conclusion the author de- clares his intention of investigating the relationships between Islamic and Western (Christian) institu- tions, but that subject is so vast and difficult that he was obliged to postpone its discussion until later. A long bibliography, Arabic and Western, is added, but we miss a geographical index which would en- able the reader to find out the references to each institution. G. S. tKHALDUN] IBN CHALDUN. (Part I, 1382-1399 A.D.; Part 2, 1399-14II A.D.) Translated from the Arabic annals of Abu-l-Mahasin Ibn Taghr; Birdi. (University of California Publications in Semitic Philology, volumes I3, I4.) xxii + 206 pp.; x + 220 pp. Berkeley and Los Angeles: University of California Press; Lon- don: Cambridge University Press, 1954. $2.50 each volume. The author of these volumes is not a stranger to our readers. They may know him as the editor of the Arabic text of one of the great Arabic chronicles, the one written by the Egyptian Abu-l-Mahasin Ibn Taghei Birdi, a man of Turkish race, who spent the whole of his life (1411-1469) in Cairo. Ibn Taghri Birdi's chronicle deals with the years 641 to 1469, and is especially valuable for the period covered by his own experience, either direct or indirect, 1382- 1469, the period of the Burjia Mamalik from Bar- qiiq to Qa' it-bey. The edition of the Arabic text was begun by the Dutchman F. G. Tuynboll in I855 and continued by Popper from 1909 to 1942 (ISS,</page><page sequence="50">i 6o ISLAMIC &amp; RELATED CULTURES 1948, 39: 8i). Having devoted the best part of his life to the edition of the Arabic text, he is now pre- paring an English translation, of which the first two volumes (1382-141I) are before us. The notes are reduced to the strict minimum, because the transla- tor is contemplating a third gift, a volume of studies which will explain all that one needs to know about the period (history, geography, administration, cal- endar, weights and measures, coinage and exchange, etc. In an age of chaos, of desultory scholarship and meretricious work, Dr. Popper is offering us an admirable example of concentration and tenacity. He will be remembered as long as Ibn Taghri Birdi, and much longer. Our readers do not know him only because of his great edition, but also be- cause of his study of the Cairo Nilometer (Isis, I95I, 43: I6i), and because of the splendid Festschrift which was dedicated to him to celebrate his seventy- fifth birthday (Isis, 1951, 43: I6o). May he live long enough to complete his immense task. G.S. ROSENTHAL, FRANZ. A history of Muslim historiography. xii + 558 pp. Leiden: Brill, 1952. Reviewed by George Sarton in Isis, 1953, 44: 78-79. Tetuin: Dar al-tabi'a al maghriblya, 1953. This is the second volume of the author's treatise on the life and work of Lisan al-din Ibn al-Khatib, to which the Miulay al-Hasan Prize was awarded in 1948. Ibn al-Khatib was one of the greatest Mus- lim scholars of Spain, and the last great one. He was born near Granada in 1313, became a high official of the Nasri court, and finally a wazir, but he shared the vicissitudes of his sovereign Muham- mad V and was finally driven into exile in Morocco. He was murdered in his prison in Fas in I374. For more details, see my Introduction (3: I762-1764). This volume is divided into 21 chapters discuss- ing various aspects of Ibn al-Khatlib's philosophy and ethics, his sources, style, his patriotism, etc. The two volumes, published under the auspices of the Institute Miilay al-HIasan in Tetuan (Spanish Morocco), are a credit to it as well as to its authors. "Of all the learned works of Abui Ijanifa ad- Dinawari (d. about 282/895) the most popular one is undoubtedly his Kitab an-nabdt. A catalogue of incunabula in the Wellcome Historical Medical Library. xv + i6o pp., I2 pL. London: Oxford Univer- sity Press, 1954. $I3.50. "The size and character of the collection [of in- cunabula] will probably be a surprise to many, for outside the great national libraries it is one of the largest in the country, and is indeed one of the largest in any medical library in the world.... Here are riches indeed for the medical historian, but they represent only a small, if important, part of the wealth of medical literature which Wellcome brought together in London. The complete cata- logue of the Wellcome Historical Medical Library, which contains over 200,000 printed books and pamphlets, as well as 5,ooo manuscripts and about I00,000 autograph letters and other signed docu- ments, is now in preparation. Its eventual publica- tion should prove a fitting memorial to the Library's founder and to his immense service to medical and historical studies, of which this present catalogue is but a token.... "Each item [in this catalogue] is described in a short-title entry, consisting of author's name or other heading, the title by which the work is most generally known, and the imprint. Reference is made to the standard and authoritative catalogues and bibliographies of fifteenth century books, for these cover the field so completely that it is con- sidered unnecessary in a catalogue of this kind to give the full bibliographical collation and transcrip- tions of incipits and colophons." Pr. mid., I953, 6r: 897-898. COMAS, JUAN. Influencia indigena en la medicina hipocritica, en la Nueva Espania del siglo XVI. America Indigena, I954, 14: 327- 36I. In this well-documented study, Dr. Comas shows the evident influence of the medicines used by the Indians of New Spain on the medical works of the Spaniards in Mexico in the XVIth century. The basis for this study is the Tractado de Medi- cina written by Dr. Fray Agustin Farfan, and pub- lished in '579 (with 3 later editions). As the author's bibliography presents great lacunae and contradictions, especially with regard to Farfan's origin and professional preparation, Dr. Comas de- votes the first part of his paper to a detailed study of numerous sources of information and arrives at the conclusion that Fray Agustin Farfan was a Spaniard, who was born in 1532 or 153I, and died in i604. There is no proof that Farfin was a professor of medicine at the University of Mexico, and as for his medical degree it is extremely doubt- ful whether he acquired it in Mexico in 1567 (at which time there was no Faculty of Medicine) or whether he achieved it in Seville before starting his voyage to New Spain. The second part of the study presents in detail, 59 remedies, largely vegetable in origin and un- doubtedly of pre-Cortesian provenience, which Far- fin included in his Tractado. Dr. Comas endeavors to give the Spanish synonym, the scientific deter- mination, and transcribes the manner of applying the remedy as indicated by Farf&amp;in. Also induded are the folios of the original in which each of the remedies is cited, often with great frequency. In synthesis, this study is a preliminary contribu- tion to the knowledge of a case of inverse accultura- tion; that is to say, a cultural feature of a conquered people (considered of inferior civilization) which was rapidly adopted by the conquerors. To this day certain medical remedies of the Mexican Indian continue in use after physiological, botanical and pharmacological investigations have scientifically proven their beneficent therapeutical effects. KEPLER, JOHANNES. Der Mensch und die Sterne-Aus seinen Werken und Briefen. Auswahl und Nachwort von Martha List. Insel-Biucherei Nr. 576. 64 pp. Insel-Verlag, I953. Miss Martha List, who has worked closely with Professor Max Caspar, has produced an attractive selection of revealing quotations from Kepler's writings. Many have appeared in Professor Cas- par's biography. For each quotation the work or letter which contains it is indicatcd. Varying in length from two lines to five pages (from the dedication of the Mysterium Cosmographicum), they all together give a delightful picture of Kepler the man and his attitude toward astronomy. He stands forth as a deeply religious and mystic individual, a man who realizes his own abilities but is willing to listen to others, past and present; a man conscious of a mission he must perform for mankind; a man with truly human qualities of humor and under- standing, who believes man is created in God's image. The concluding essay unifies the booklet, and there is also an index-altogether a charming addition to any library. C. D. H. KEPLER, JOHANNES, Epitome of Copernican astronomy. KOYRt, ALEXANDRE. An experiment in measurement. Proc. Amer. Phil. Soc., I953, 97: 223-237. "The moral of this history of the determination of the acceleration constant is thus rather curious. We have seen Galileo, Mersenne, Riccioli endeavor- ing to construct a timekeeper in order to be able to make an experimental measure of the speed of the fall. We have seen Huygens succeed, where his predecessors had failed, and, by his very success, dispense with making the actual measurement. This, because his timekeeper is, so to say, a measure- ment in itself; the determination of its exact period is already a much more precise and refined experi- ment than all those that Mersenne and Riccioli have ever thought of. The meaning and value of the Huygensian circuit- which finally revealed itself as a shortcut-is therefore clear: not only are good experiments based upon theory, but even the means to perform them are nothing else than theory incarnate." "The lack of interest in his writings was partly due to the unnecessarily abstract mathematical form in which he expresses his ideas, partly to the prolix style, in which all kinds of secondary interests are interpolated in the stream of the main ideas, such as the discussion of the relation of the affinities to the combining masses . . . . His peculiar attitude towards the phlogiston theory and his attempt to combine it with Lavoisier's system, although based on real problems which were not adequately at- tended to by the followers of Lavoisier, were little to the taste of the new generation of chemists, and his awkward nomenclature and symbolism, based on antiquated ideas, made his works very difficult to read . . . . Richter belongs eminently to this class: some of his ideas are quite incorrect, but he nevertheless arrived at one of the fundamental laws of chemical combination, for which he is entitled to ungrudging credit." FRANKENBERGER, Z. Jan Krtitel BohAR [I724-I768]. Zivot a dilo. (In Czech, sum- mary in English.) Vistnik Krdlovski Zesk6 spolecnosti nauk. Tfida matematicko-prirodo- vedecki. [I949] I950, I-I22. "The most important feature of this zoological work of Bohac consists in detailed and exhaustive study of the observed animals; in that respect he appears as opponent of the 'new method' of Linne who regards as the first purpose of zoology the giving of an appropriate specific name to every animal, the characterization of it by as short a diagnosis as possible, and inserting it in its right place in the system. BohAZ, on the contrary, postu- lates from zoology an exhaustive description of the internal anatomy as well, a study of the physio- logical significance of the observed organs, and ob- servations on the mode of life of the animals studied. In another place he defines zoology as fol- lows: Est enim Zoologia ex historia naturali pars, quae non solum juxta certos characteres extrinsecos omnia animalia denominare, in certas classes, or- dines, genera et species dividere docet; sed etiam eorum propretates, noxas et varios usus recenset." [GOETHE, JOHANN WOLFGANG von.] Goe- the's botanical writings. Translated by Bertha Mueller, with an introduction by Charles J. Engard. x + 258 pp., portrait, 5i figs. Hono- lulu: University of Hawaii Press, 1952. $5.00. This collection contains Goethe's major and minor writings on botanical subjects grouped as follows: "On morphology," "On his plant studies," "On general theory." It is useful to have these essays in an English version. The bibliography lists a few works in English, but none in other languages (see Schmid's bibliography, Isis, s95I, 42: 33I-332). Most scholars know of Goethe's famous Meta- morphosis of plants, but few of them are aware of his other botanical works. Bertha Mueller has here translated them all in a well arranged and well edited collection, valuable to students of Goethe, to botanists and to historians of science. C. Z. HAGBERG, KNUT. I. B. C. STERN, CURT; GERTRUD BELAR. Race crossing in Paradise? J. Hered., I953, 44: 154- 55. On a door panel in the Alte Apotheke in the town of Calw in Southern Germany is a painting of Adam and Eve in the Garden of Eden. Adam is depicted as a white man but Eve is black. The authors suggest that, if all the races of men were descended from Adam and Eve, their types should be represented in our first parents, or so it ap- peared to the artist. Religio Medici, edited from the manuscript copies and the early edi- tions by Jean-Jacques Denonain. Xhi + I20 pp. Cambridge: at the University Press, 1953. 25S. "The aim of this edition is to settle the text of Religio Medici in the light of new or recon- sidered evidence and as objectively as possible, and not according to incomplete data or even biased opinions, as was the case with some previous edi- tors. The edition gathers and records in exhaustive critical notes the various readings of the manu- script and printed evidence- a task never yet ade- quately undertaken. Secondly, this is an attempt to reconsider the editing problem as a whole.... Eight manuscript copies of Religio Medici are known to exist at the present time. None of them was actually written by the author himself, as com- parison of the handwritings easily shows." BROZOVIC', LEANDER. Biographisches ilber Joseph Franz Domin. Centaurus, 1951, 2: 38- 43. De Castro Sarmento (I692-1762) was a Portuguese-Jewish physician, who obtained his doctor's degree in Coimbra in 1717, became rabbi of the Portuguese Jews in London. The pragmatic phi- losophy of C. S. Peirce. xvii + 3I8 pp. Chi- cago: The University of Chicago Press, 953. $5.75. The plan of the volume may be inferred from its outline. It consists of an introduction and six chapters. These are divided into two parts. In the first part there is a chapter on "New Logic and a New List of Categories"; a chapter on "The Facul- ties of Knowledge"; and a chapter on "The Meth- ods of Obtaining Knowledge." The second part is devoted to chapters on "Scientific Metaphysics"; on "The Order of the Sciences"; and finally, on "Prag- matic Philosophy." The aim of the book is to serve as an exposition of, as well as a commentary on, the philosophy of Charles S. Peirce. However, it turns out to be more than that. Professor Thompson wishes to prove that it is really a pragmatic philosophy, contrary to the stated intention of Peirce himself (p. 202). C. E. TRESSE, R. George Davidson (I825-19II), born in Eng- land, educated in Philadelphia, began his scientific career in the magnetic observatory established by Alexander Dallas Bache in Girard College. Famous for his work in the U. S. Coast Survey, charting of the Pacific coastline from Alaska to Mexico, the Coast Pilot was known as "Davidson's Bible." He "established and maintained for many years the first astronomical observatory on the Pacific Coast, and it was mostly through his influence that the San Francisco capitalist James Lick provided the funds to build the great observatory that bears his name. He headed transit of Venus expeditions to Japan and Corro Roblero and, in I867, con- ducted a geographical reconnaissance of Alaska and urged its purchase from Russia. . . . In I870 he was appointed Professor of Geodesy and Astronomy at the University of California, served as Regent from I877 to I884, and in I895 became Professor of Geography, which post he held until shortly before his death in I9 I." VANBREUSEGHEM, R. Maurice Langeron (1874-I950) (Biographie et Bibliographie). Mycopath. et. Mycol. applicata, i95i, 6: 58- 64. Portr. WAKSMAN, SELMAN A. Sergei N. Wino- gradsky, his life and work: The story of a great bacteriologist. xiii + I50 pp. New Brunswick, N. J.: Rutgers University Press, I953- The author, a Nobel laureate, has written an ap- preciative biography of his friend, the great Russian bacteriologist, Winogradsky (I856-i953). Wino- gradsky is best known for his work on the auto- tropic bacteria, the free living bacteria which fix nitrogen, and the sulfur bacteria. Born in Kiev into a wealthy land-owning family, he worked in St. Petersburg (i88i-i884), in Strassburg, under the guidance of Anton De Bary (i885-i888), Zurich (I888-I895) and again in St. Petersburg (I89I- 1905) as director of the Institute of Experimental Medicine. At the age of 49, Winogradsky retired to his country estates, but the Russian revolution displaced him and in 1922, at the age of 66, he again became active in his science, this time in Paris, where he was associated with the Pasteur Institute. He retired finally in 194I at the age of 85, and died in 1953. Sergei N. Winogradsky consists of a brief biog- raphy (pp. I-86), a selected correspondence and a bibliography (pp. 87-150), three portraits of the subject act. ca. 32, 67, and 8o, and several informal pictures. C. Z. WRIGHT, ALBERT HAZEN. Agassiz and Cornell. (Pre-Cornell and Early Cornell I, Studies in History no. I5.) 5I pp., plates. Ithaca, A. H. Wright, II3 E. Upland Rd.: I953. $135 paper. f. Sciences of man (psychology, cultural anthropology, sociology) FREUD, SIGMUND. The origins of psycho- analysis, letters to Wilhelm Fliess, drafts and notes: I887-I902. Edited by Marie Bona- parte, Anna Freud, Ernst Kris. Authorized translation by Eric Mosbacher and James Strachey. Introduction by Ernst Kris. xi + 486 pp. New York: Basic Books, Inc., I954. $6.75. "This book consists of a selection of letters from Sigmund Freud to Wilhelm Fliess, a Berlin physi- cian and biologist, written between the years 1887 and i1902 . . . . The author of the material in this volume would not have consented to the publica- tion of any of it. It was Freud's habit to destroy all notes and preliminary drafts as soon as they had served their purpose, to publish nothing incomplete or unfinished, and to publish material of a personal nature only when it was essential for the purpose of demonstrating unconscious connections. These letters were brought to light by chance, and the editors feel justified in publishing them in spite of the hesitation which respect for the author's attitude in the matter inevitably imposes. They amplify the prehistory and early history of psycho-analysis in a way that no other available material does, provide insight into certain phases of Freud's intellectual processes from his first clinical impressions until the formulation of his theory, throw light on the blind alleys and wrong roads into which he was diverted in the process of hypothesis-building, and furnish a vivid picture of him during the difficult years during which his interest shifted from physiology and neurology to psychology and psychopathology." "The School of the Hospital de la Charite, according to De Courmelles as a repre- sentative of this group, functioned within a three- fold orientation: hypnotism, magnetism, and sug- gestion. The experimental and physicalist approach paralleled the Sa}petriere School. The therapeutic and suggestion concepts were in keeping with the Nancy School. The total impression was one of leaning more toward the Charcot faction at Sal- petriere. Post-Mesmeric fluidist concepts appear to have been retained. The chief investigator of the Charite School was Luys whose name remains with us today, although he never matched the stature of Charcot as a neurologist nor of Liebeault or Bernheim as hypnotists. His revolving mirror is still remembered . . . . Whether the Charite School was recognized during its existence as dis- tinct a group as De Courmelles seems to imply is not quite clear, and further study should clarify this too. B. C.</page><page sequence="93">CONTEMPORARY SCIENCE (GENERAL - PHYSICAL SCIENCES) 203 HEISENBERG, WERNER. Philosophic prob- lems of nuclear science. Eight lectures, trans- lated by F. C. Hayes. I 26 pp. London, Faber and Faber, 1952. i6s. Reviewed by G. P. Thomson in Endeavour, 1953, 12: io5-io6. KRAFT, VICTOR. The Vienna Circle. The Origin of Neo-Positivism, a chapter in the his- tory of recent philosophy. Translated from the German by Arthur Pap. Xii + 209 pp. New York: Philosophical Library, 1953. $3.75. Victor Kraft's brief history of the Vienna Circle and of the achievement of its principle members is a primer of positivism written for a German audi- ence which has, to date, ignored or uncritically re- jected positivist opinion. The original filled a gap in the German philosophical literature; since Eng- lish publication shows no comparable lacuna, the translation will have a more limited function. The eight pages which constitute "Part One" are a use- ful summary of the organization of the Circle, its relations with the major European and American positivists, and the Congresses and publications through which its doctrines won international at- tention. The summary covers the period from Schlick's accession, in 1922, to the "chair for philosophy of the inductive sciences" founded for Ernst Mach to the permanent dissolution of the group by the Anschituss in 1938. The one hun- dred and eighty pages of "Part Two" provide an elementary account of the central doctrines of major positivist thinkers. Though the exposition follows Carnap's views somewhat too closely, more justice is done to the diversity of positivist opinion and to the range of unsolved problems than is usual in comparably elementary accounts. Yet the at- tempt to represent historical growth and diversity in so limited a compass detracts from the persuasive- ness of the account without compensating by pro- viding increased insight into the basic philosophical problems which confront the group internally and externally. Nuclear physics. ix + 225 pp., i8 halftone and 34 line ill. New York: Philosophical Library, I953. $4.75. Based on lectures originally delivered to the Association of German Electrical Engineers, this book is an admirable introduction to nuclear physics, in historical perspective. Like many physicists, Heisenberg conceives the history of atomism to jump from Democritus to Gassendi, with Aristotle continuously "incontestable" in the intervening two millenia. Other chestnuts are the attribution of the modern definition of element to Boyle, Lavoisier's prediction (verified by experi- ment) that in combustion "the body must be- come heavier." The controversial article on "Re- search in Germany on the technical application of atomic energy," published in Die Naturwis- senschaften and in Nature is reprinted in an ap- pendix; the opposite point of view (on whether the German failure to produce an atomic bomb stemmed from a policy of concentrating on "the utilization of nuclear energy in prime movers") has been presented by S. Index of Names Included in the Eightieth Critical Bibliography of the History of Science The Index contains all the proper names cited (not merely those of authors, editors and trans- lators) with the exception of reviewers. The name of a person who lived in the Middle Ages or the Renaissance might appear under several forms: that of the vernacular language; the name under which he wrote; the Latinized form; or, in the case of certain well known names, an Anglicized version. For example, JOHN OF SPAIN might appear thus, or as IBN DAWUD, or JuAN HIsPANo, or JOANNES HISPALENSIS. IBN RUSHD might appear thus, or as AvERRots, the Latinized form. Such linguistic variations as HiRACLITE or ERAcLITo have been avoided in favor of HERACLITUS, and HOm1ERE, though it may appear thus in a work cited in French, will be found in the Index as HOMER. In general, however, we have aimed at coherence by indexing the name under the most commonly known form; but readers are urged to search under all the variously used versions.
